0312576463|4.0|The fact that I read almost 600 pages of small print in about a week, even though I only had two days off for Christmas, tells me that I found this novel to be very entertaining and engrossing. The first chapter had me hooked. It was so well-written, I would call it virtuosic. However, I feel that the rest of the book, although very well-written from cover to cover, doesn't quite maintain the level of excellence of the first chapter. This novel isn't the equal of a Middlemarch, although that's what it seems to be striving for. Middlemarch strikes the perfect balance between engrossing soap opera and consciousness-raising social commentary, whereas Frantzen adds a little too much soap opera to the recipe. Also, there's a subdued but persistent undercurrent of misanthropy running throughout the novel that turns me off, even as it strikes a deep chord within me. I know all too well that it's difficult for intellectuals who care about the world and the environment to avoid a smug, patronizing, contemptuous attitude towards people who may be poorly educated and misguided in their opinions and habits, but it seems to me that some of our greatest writers (Saramago and Welty are just a couple of examples) transcend that attitude and are able to write with deep empathy, humanism, and compassion about ignorant, misguided, selfish, or peasant-like people. But I'm sure that if I suddenly gained Frantzen's writing talent due to some freak gamma ray accident, my own novels would betray the same kind of misanthropy. I do find it amusing that Walter's politics are pretty much exactly like mine, and some of his speeches about overpopulation and economic growth remind me almost uncannily of rants I've delivered to friends and acquaintances. I wonder how much Walter serves as a mouthpiece for Frantzen's own opinions or if he's poking fun at Walter's politics as much as he is at Walter's personality and social skills. One last minor gripe: I find the descriptions of music and its milieu slightly off (major and minor power chords?). I think maybe Frantzen is too detailed in those sections for his own good, or maybe it's my own views about music that are slightly off. But these are minor reservations that didn't impede my enjoyment of this rich, ambitious, and entertaining novel.

B0041KKYEM|4.0|The greatness of Black Swan lies in its power to evoke. This review is sort of about all the other reviews. Like a flawed gemstone, what you see depends upon what facet you look at. Reviews that love Black Swan or hate it show the reviewers often going through a process of struggle to come to grips with Black Swan and express their judgments. Director Darren Aronofsky drives a stiletto into your mind with his vast store of cinematic tricks; your reaction may be negative. Deciding what is reality puts your mind in an uproar. Because of the constant close camera work on Natalie Portman, I felt the film was emotionally grueling while visually enthralling. The ending is a head-spinner. How well you can tolerate ambiguity, disturbing images, multiple themes, and so on, will determine whether you like it or not. Overall, I thought Black Swan was a difficult, but compelling experience.<br /><br />The film rests mainly on the phenomenal artistic control displayed by Natalie Portman. Her gradual slide from White Swan to Black is a tremendous acting achievement. Her physical effort in the role of Nina is reminiscent of Robert DeNiro's \\"Raging Bull\\". Her performance draws strength from a superb supporting cast headed by Vincent Cassel, Barbara Hershey, Winona Ryder, and Mila Kunis. Others have discussed technical aspects of Black Swan better than I can.<br /><br />Before I say what I think Black Swan is about, let me say that Black Swan operates on many levels at once. You can say it's art house porn and be correct, and say it is a meditation on the pursuit of perfection and also be correct. Here the film's ambiguity wraps itself around you and starts to squeeze.<br /><br /> Black Swan is encoded with all sorts of symbols and images (particularly mirrors) you may pick up on. Some are blatantly, crudely obvious, like the winged tattoo on rival Lily's back (Mila Kunis). Others, like the rave scene in the nightclub, are not apparent unless you stop-frame the sequence and see justwhat is spilling out of Nina's psyche. Things Lily says to Nina are often prophetic.<br /><br />The opening dance/dream sequence defines Nina's baseline mental state: virginal innocence that flees from sexuality by transforming into a safe, pure White Swan. Compare that sequence with dance director Thomas' (Vincent Cassel) sexually harassing Nina later on to get Nina to express passion in her dancing, and you get a sense that there is a lot of sophisticated overlapping going on between Nina's mental state and the Swan Lake storyline.<br /><br />When Nina finishes performing the Black Swan coda in the film's finale, we see two enormous winged shadows behind her as she basks in triumph before a wild audience. This suggests maybe she has reached some sort of spiritual equilibrium between her light and dark side while also being totally insane. Other clues are in her name: 'Nina\\" means 'little girl' in Spanish, as other reviewers have noted. Also, 'Nina' could be a nod to prima ballerina Nina Ananiashvili, as perfect a ballerina as there ever was. When Nina goes to work, in the film's beginning, she is wrapped in feathery, downy neck warmers. The film is rich with possible clues to completing the Nina puzzle.<br /><br />Okay, so what is Black Swan really about? It's about a 'chick' desperately trying to break out of her shell so she can flap her wings and leave her mother's nest. Remember, Black Swan is a flawed gem. Turn it another way you will see something else.

0201633612|5.0|As others have already noted, this is a seminal work on design patterns and is considered by many software professionals as a must read. It is probably a bit too complex for novices to design patterns in which case they are better off using another resource for starters (e.g.: Head First Design Patterns) and then eventually move on to this book. Head First design patterns was obviously easier to read and understand since the examples are more up to date and material is not as thorough as this book. It took me more than a month and half to read this book carefully from cover to cover. Although a lot of the patterns are discussed in depth from a theoretical level including evaluating trade-offs made with specific implementation choices, the examples in SmallTalk are out of date and not that relevant anymore but that's understandable given the publish date of this book (1994). Like some other books (e.g.: Java Concurrency in Practice), multiple readings are necessary to fully digest the material and that needs to be coupled with either independent practice of the specific implementation choices and/or other resources that have more examples of pattern implementations so as to not only reinforce understanding but also lay a solid foundation for Object Oriented Design. Most senior folks working with Object Oriented Systems typically have this book at their desk as a reference. I found one particular pattern, namely Interpreter, pretty difficult to follow. Other than that the rest of the material is readable. Having some familiarity with UML notation will help but the appendix includes explanation of the notation used in the book, so it is not a stopper if you don't have any exposure in that area. Towards the end of each chapter covering a given pattern, the authors include a section on related patterns which can be extremely helpful. This book also organizes different patterns into creational, structural and behavioral categories and also identifies which ones within a given category can supplement each other and which ones compete against each other. This book is not meant to be a comprehensive resource on design patterns and will have to be supplemented by other books. Highly recommended for anyone working with Object Oriented Systems.

The acting was also great and the fear you see in the actors felt genuine.  The acting was good and I was convinced that I was behind the scenes with a small television crew.  I was also reminded of a Tales from the Crypt episode (i think one that came directly from the EC comic series actually)that is very similar.  So the idea isn't all that new, but again the cast and crew did a great job and it shows.<br /><br />Now that I've seen it, I'm kinda surprised by all the lackluster reviews it has been getting.  I'm a long time horror fan, and I felt like this was a great treatment of the genre.  In a time when horror is all over the place (It's a great time to be a fan of horror! And I hope that continues), this one was right down the middle for me.  It's a classic haunted house film at its core.  And while the course the story take makes creative turns it never tries to be something else.  Andthat's a good thing in my opinion.  It spoke to that primal fear, or unease of being alone in dark places.  It was very much like watching a terrible nightmare.  So I say give the film a chance and you might be pleasantly surprised.<br /><br />Also, it might be easy to write this off as a Paranormal Activity clone at first glance but I guarantee this is something different.  I've enjoyed a couple films in that series too, but I think this one does a little better job immersing you into the experience.  More of this film is in a first person perspective than occurs in Paranormal Activity.  You feel like your running through the corridors, hiding in rooms, and experiencing the fright alongside the crew.  (Don't get me wrong, the Paranormal Activity films are great.  I'm just trying to point out where they differ)

B005J29HCQ|5.0|The \\"Smile Sessions\\" is a perfect synthesis of psychedelic music and lyrics that, in my opinion, has no peer in the history of popular music. Period. I have never experienced an album, until now, that I can listen to every day (and I do) without it becoming tiresome. \\"Our Prayer\\" is mind-blowing. Who else but the Beach Boys could produce those harmonies? \\"Heroes and Villains\\" is astonishing in its sheer inventiveness. Brian called \\"Good Vibrations\\" a \\"pocket symphony;\\" I would call Heroes and Villains a \\"pocket opera.\\" \\"Do You Like Worms,\\" despite missing some of the vocals, is amazing in its own right. The entire first suite, beginning with \\"Our Prayer,\\" and ending with \\"Cabin Essence,\\" is elusive yet accessible. The almost buried (on purpose) vocals at the end of \\"My Only Sunshine\\" and throughout \\"Cabin Essence\\" add an element of mystery to these songs that is very cool indeed. \\"Cabin Essence\\" is an under-appreciated masterpiece that is every bit the equal of \\"Good Vibrations,\\" and should be studied and deconstructed by anyone interested in music composition. The sounds of the railroad - of \\"Who ran the iron horse\\" - are heard as you close your eyes and listen. It takes Spector's \\"wall of sound\\" to a whole new level, and every time I hear it I am left wondering: how did he do it? There's never been anything like it, and never will be. Of course, one can say that about the entire album.  \\"Wonderful\\" starts off the second suite, and is, well, wonderful; Brian's voice has never sounded more beautiful than on this and \\"Surf's Up,\\" which, if I had to pick a favorite, \\"Surf's Up\\" would be it. I love the lyrics: \\"I heard the word. Wonderful thing! A children's song. Have you listened as they play? Their song is love and the children know the way.\\" \\"Surf's Up,\\" classically structured in three parts of almost equal length (like the album), is otherworldly in its beauty from start to finish. It's hard to believe that Brian and Van Dyke wrote this and several other SMiLE songs in one night! Of course, let's not forget \\"Look\\" and \\"Child Is Father Of The Man\\" - I can't get enough listens to the latter, even though it's lacking some of the vocals and strings found on the Brian Wilson version.  Finally, we're on to the third suite, which perhaps more fully embodies the \\"divine\\" and the \\"dumb\\" of the original \\"Dumb Angel\\" title, starting with \\"I Wanna Be Around.\\" \\"Vega-Tables\\" always puts a smile on my face, and it's easy to overlook the beautiful melodies underneath the humorous lyrics (see the 45 version in the box set for the best version of this song). \\"Holidays\\" is missing some vocals but still packs a punch, especially the whispering winds ending. \\"Wind Chimes\\" consists of a lush Carl Wilson vocal and an early calm that soon gives way to a more rocking ending that is unexpected but incredible. \\"Fire,\\" part of the original Elements suite, is unlike anything you've ever heard, and is the only song I used to skip over but have lately developed an appreciation for it, as it's yet another example of how cinematic and epic this whole album is - the music always sounds like the lyrics - it's very impressionistic throughout. I love the percussion in \\"Love to Say Dada,\\" and closing the album proper is \\"Good Vibrations,\\" a piece of pop perfection that still amazes despite countless listens, and is even more powerful with the \\"hum-de-dum\\" section and the altered ending.  It took 44 years but it was worth the wait!

B002SR2PKG|4.0|So apparently there is an actual term for fear of clowns: \\"coulrophobia\\".  Well, I'm pretty sure that this novel and the subsequent movie has spawned that phobia in large numbers of people over the past couple of decades.  Clowns always had that creep factor in my mind, and they have scared the you-know-what out of me since childhood.  Naturally, reading this before bed for a couple of weeks was a less than stellar way to achieve a good night's sleep... but the book was really good.<br /><br />The story tells the tale of seven childhood friends who are all somewhat outcasts in their own right and the horrifying experiences that they endured in 1958.  Something, \\"It\\", was terrorizing their town of Derry, Maine, and children were disappearing.  Sometimes their mutilated bodies were found, and sometimes they weren't.  \\"It's\\" apparition of choice is that of a clown named Pennywise who seems to dwell in the sewers... which gives him access to each and every house and apartment in town.  But our seven protagonists find themselves as targets for the shape-shifting terror that is living and feeding off of their home, especially when they decide that the time has come to kill It.<br /><br />The story also picks up on the lives of those friends twenty-seven years later in 1985 as they all come back to Derry when It begins to start Its cycle of murder again.  Most of them have left Derry and established their adult lives elsewhere, but they made a pact as kids that they would all return to Derry if It resurfaced so they could finish what they started -- and finally kill It for good.<br /><br />The book doesn't tell the story chronologically; instead, King jumps back and forth from 1958 to 1985, often in mid-sentence.  It can be a little difficult to follow at times (particularly if you're searching for a stopping point), but it brilliantly juxtaposes events from the past and present.  Since the book is so long, this method of writing does help to keep the different happenings and incidents from both time periods fresh in your mind, and both sides of each character remain in the forefront of your mind.  So while at times it may take an extra moment or two to navigate, I think it's very effective in the long run.<br /><br />I was listening to an interview with Stephen King not too long ago, and he mentioned that when he writes, he does so with one key question in his mind: \\"What is the worst possible thing that could happen?\\"  Well, if everything in this book happened to someone, I'm pretty sure it couldn't get much worse.  Mission accomplished!  I was already jumpy after reading about this psychopathic clown, but when he brought my other fear into it -- a spider, thanks for that -- my nerves were pretty much shot.<br /><br />This wasn't my favorite Stephen King book, but it's still a great read!  Fair warning: you may be afraid of your bathroom for a while. B001BANK32|5.0|One of the reasons I respect Stephen King so much is the breadth of his books.  His storylines are so unique that there is certain to be a book that grabs each of us.  I think that's why so many people cite different books as their favorite King novel!  For me, \\"The Shining\\" is it (although I always enjoy reading his other stuff to see if there is something that can actually top this).<br /><br />I will preface this by saying that the book is very different than the Stanley Kubrick film.  They are two very different stories, although both brilliant when taken as they are and not lumped together.  The movie is one of my favorites, and I actually watched it before reading the book.  However, the novel is at the top of my list as well!<br /><br />The book begins with Jack Torrance interviewing for a position as the winter caretaker at the Overlook Hotel which is nestled high in the mountains of Colorado.  The hotel closes every winter as it is just short of impossible to access due to the weather, and there is a need for someone to stay there in order to make repairs, rotate heat throughout the hotel, and perform other preventive maintenance.  For Jack, this seems perfect.  Financially, things have been tough, and as a writer, he could greatly use those months to finish his work without much distraction.  When he gets the job, he settles in for the snowy season with his wife, Wendy, and son, Danny.<br /><br />The character development of Jack has a huge effect on the entire book.  Jack moves into the Overlook with a lot of personal baggage.  He is an on-the-wagon alcoholic whose tornadic past resulted in unemployment, a strained marriage, and a heavy sense of parental guilt.  His personal demons trace back to his childhood, and it's evident that his self-confidence was flushed down the toilet a while back.  Although somewhat reluctantly, he recognizes the Overlook as an opportunity for redemption: it provides employment, free room and board, a chance to get his writing career back on track, and an environment where there is absolutely no alcohol.  His only problem is that the Overlook has a much different agenda.<br /><br />The book differs from the movie in that the true villain is the Overlook itself.  This is the epitome of haunted house stories.  There are ghosts, there is a dark history, and there is evil incarnate living and breathing in the walls of the hotel itself.  The Overlook sees Jack's vulnerabilities and exploits them.<br /><br />The Overlook's true character is primarily viewed through the eyes of two of the main characters: Jack and his son, Danny.  Young Danny has what is called \\"the shining\\".  He can see things (among other abilities), and the Overlook throws this poor kid's sixth sense into overdrive.  He witnesses gruesome scenes of violence that have happened over the years in the hotel.  And as the winter progresses, he too becomes the target of the Overlook's attention, although in a very different way than his father.<br /><br />And poor Wendy is just pulling her hair out trying to figure out what's wrong with her husband and her son.<br /><br />Not only is the book classically haunting, but there is a sense of mystery as the history of the Overlook is pieced together and as its intentions are revealed.  The hotel really is as much a main character in the book as Jack, Danny, or Wendy.<br /><br />Perhaps one of the most striking themes in the book is the true isolation of Jack, Wendy, and Danny.  As their horror story unfolds, it really hits home that there is no escaping this situation.  When you read that it's only Thanksgiving, you realize that they have four months to go before the hotel reopens.  There are those constant questions of \\"how much worse can it get?\\" and \\"how much longer can they last?\\"  Ironically, in a recent Stephen King interview, he said that when he writes, he always keeps this question in mind: What is the worst possible thing that could happen?  Well, that should give you an ideaof just how bad things go for these three people!<br /><br />This isn't one of Stephen King's longer novels, so the events escalate at a more rapid rate.  Thus, it's gripping from the start, is very suspenseful, and is a quick read.<br /><br />On an exciting note, Stephen King recently announced that he is currently writing a sequel!  I won't write the premise here since it will give a little spoiler to the end of this book, but you can search it online. 0756405890|3.0|Yes, this novel is readable and has its entertaining moments. But ultimately, it left me saying that I shouldn't be surprised that this is Rothfuss' debut novel.<br /><br />Let's start with the fact that the author spoils most of the dramatic tension of the book from the very beginning. After plodding through the first 50 pages -- by far the novel's worst -- we learn that our protagonist is a contemporary hero hiding himself as an innkeeper in a tiny village. He is found out by \\"the Chronicler\\" and decides to tell him his story, to clear up all the legends floating about. Thereby, we learn that this much storied hero has grown old, grown timid, and worst of all: has survived the perils of the world.<br /><br />Isn't the point of reading a good fantasy novel that we get to adventure alongside our hero as she or he battles demons, dragons, and the machinations of the heart -- all the while, wondering if they'll survive the worst of it or die in the arms of a friend or once-endangered lover? Instead, our author has already taken all the fun out of every awful encounter in the book as we know with perfect certainty that our hero will make it through, unscathed, to eventually make a dubious real estate purchase. Thrilling.<br /><br />Of all the recent fantasy, this book shares the most tropes with Harry Potter -- dead parents, gifted child, school for magicians, eccentric professors, etc. -- but unfortunately shares none of the wit or cleverness. And while we readers raced breathlessly through every uncertain Voldemort encounter toward the inevitable series climax, wondering which of the two would survive (only one can!!), here we're left with none of the mystery or suspense as each rising action is broken by an interlude back at the dreary Waystone Inn, where our protagonist forces the Chronicler to slowly learn how every fantastic story told about great deeds was actually just a turn of magic little better than a long-practiced card trick.<br /><br />These \\"flash-forwards\\"/interludes are so ham-fisted and begrudging that I can't help but wonder if they're the work of an inexperienced editor who demanded a \\"greater\\" structure to the story before it could be sold.<br /><br />For all its faults, I'll reiterate that it's not an /awful/ book... but that's a rather low bar to cross, isn't it? There were some enjoyable moments that I often met with uncertainty, wondering if there was just a little too much wish-fulfillment going on. I do long to learn the answers to a few well-placed mysteries that will undoubtedly be explored in later books, but I'm just not sure if my curiosity is worth the next 993 pages of foregone conclusions...grudging that I can't help but wonder if they're the work of an inexperienced editor who demanded a \\"greater\\" structure to the story before it could be sold. After receiving unexpected kindness from a bishop he robs, Jean Valjean makes the decision to break his parole and start a new life.  However, his decision incurs relentless pursuit from the stern yet, ultimately, tormented Inspector Javert.  Jean Valjean will be forced on a journey in which he'll meet unforgettable characters and make momentous decisions that will effect many lives.<br /><br />This 25th anniversary concert is a true delight from start to finish. B000LC4TJ0|3.0|Approaching this interpretation of one of the best operas ever written, with such a star-studded cast, one holds his breath in anticipation.  It has been a perennial favorite since its grandiose triumph in Prague in 1786, after nine performances in Vienna at its premiere; since those times Le Nozze remains part of the most popular repertoire.  There are many reasons for it.<br /><br />It's been four years since Mozart's breaking off with his abusive hereditary employer archbishop Colloredo in Salzburg, a character resembling in his feudal attitudes Il Conte Almaviva.  Mozart's arrival in Vienna then in 1782  was marked by the composition of his first opera as a freelance composer - Die Entf&uuml;hrung aus dem Serail, premiered in 1782 to a huge success.  By now, in 1786, Mozart was a well-established composer in Vienna, brimming with energy and creativity, very successful with instrumental works and concerts, but wishing again to astonish the public with a great opera made on a fashionable and somewhat scandalous subject regarding the conflict of aristocrasy and the others.  The French Revolution ideas are already circling over Paris, Vienna and progressive European capitals, the terrible uprising is only a few years away, but at the moment all the ruin, bloodshed and horrors are not foreseen, and the daring dangerous satire is extremely popular.  The Figaro  trilogy by Pierre-Augustin de Beaumarchais is now complete with the first two parts:  Le Barbier de S&eacute;ville ou la Pr&eacute;caution inutile (written in 1772, first performed in 1775) and Le mariage de Figaro, ou La folle journee (completed and first performed in 1784).  Both works were a great hit in Europe, and it was Mozart's idea to set the drama to music.  He handled the Beaumarchais play to Lorenzo da Ponte and the work on the libretto started.<br /><br />It is quite amazing that Mozart and da Ponte had decided to use as a base the material banned by Emperor - Joseph II has forbidden the play in Austro-Hungarian Empire. However, an opera was allowed to be done and performed!  Why did he do it?  There are a few explanations, all of which are not very convincing - including the one that the Emperor wanted to teach the nobility the political lesson that a servant can assert his rights against his master.  However, it seems that such liberal policies that allowed the mockery of the existing regime were exactly the reasons for the collapse of monarchies, starting with the Fremch Revolution, in which Joseph II's sister met an atrocious end.  Why were liberal monarchs, patrons of the arts, so short-sighted?  The questions remains an enigma, but the good thing is that Emperor's patronage made it possible for us enjoy this opera.<br /><br />Le Nozze had a predesessor - as mentioned, it is the second part of the Figaro trilogy; however the first part Le Barbier de S&eacute;ville was set to music by by Giovanni Paisiello to an opera  Il barbiere di Siviglia, ovvero La precauzione inutile (The Barber of Seville, or The Useless Precaution).  The opera was premiered in 1782  at the Imperial Court, Saint Petersburg.  It is curious to note that the same libretto was reused by Gioacchino Rossini for his masterpiece, in 1816, with then old Paisiello, having lost his position as a privileged court composer, witnessing such an irritating development.  Paisiello was born before Mozart - in 1740, and died long after the French Revolution and Mozart - in 1816, and one can only imagine what he must have had to say about all he had seen in his eventful life, which encompassed epochs from Louis XV to Napoleon, with Catherine the Great in between.<br /><br />Now, with a such a trail of an extremely rich history, what can be said of the performance in question?  Perhaps we can start with the good things - and to me, the conducting was an absolute perfection.  Nikolaus Harnoncourt followed the great tradition of Herbert von Karajan, and when listening to Karajan after this Harnoncourt, one can hear an unmistakable likeness in the approach:<br /><br />[[ASIN:B000TEPM5U Mozart: Le Nozze di Figaro]]<br /><br />I love the tempi, the unhurried spirit, the phrasing and the pauses - Harnoncourt is very sensitive to give singers an opportunity to sing, and they do it so well until the overall stage direction interferes to ruin the great conducting.  But we still concentrate on the positives, so next plus will be again Harnoncourt's in-depth respect for the score - for example, both arias that are sometimes omitted in Act IV  (as they are in Karl Bohm's otherwise excellent interpretation [[ASIN:B0007P0LNO Mozart - Le Nozze di Figaro (The Marriage of Figaro)]]) are preserved - Marcellina's \\"Il capro e la capretta\\" and Basilio's \\"In quegli anni in cui val poco\\".  Regarding the latter, the story is even more interesting - a tenor sings this aria in this performance - Don Curzio/Oliver Ringelhahn; this must be a tribute to the first Basilio - an Irish tenor Michael Kelly, who sang both Basilio and Don Curzio at the premier in 1786.  Today Basilio is sung more by baritones, as is the case with this production, but the aria is preserved in its tenor appearance.<br /><br />About the performers - surely Anna Netrebko is the main focus here; she is in a perfect form and is a delight to watch and listen.  Yet Susanna has only one aria, and the role when it comes to pure singing is not as rich as the Countess; Netrebko is a great Susanna but the overall dull production does not let her shine to her fullest.<br /><br />Figaro (Ildebrando D'Arcangelo) is convincing in his singing, but again the ravages of the production dull his performance.  Hermann Prey had set a standard, with a cunning witty character, and a serious and slow-minded Figaro is not what can make an audience smile.<br /><br />Bo Skovhus is a good Count, he sings expertly - I saw him in Arabella in Wiener Staatsoper in March 2011, he was singing Mandryka, a role requiring the same type of voice, and also of a country aristocrat.  He was very good, but here as there his manner is somewhat reserved; the oddities of this production affected his performance in a negative way; in any case, I wish to see a swaggering, proud Count - then he is amusing in his miseries; but this image is just a hopeless loser, which is not too entertaining.<br /><br />Cherubino with Christine Shafer is marvelous, even if not too charming.  Her singing expiates for any flaws in appearance - that is, she is not a sweet and adorable young boy-doll with whom every lady wants to play.<br /><br />Now, the not so good points:<br /><br />The stage director found it necessary to introduce Ibsen and Strindberg into the libertine opera buffa.  What can possibly be a more lunatic idea?  The result is this giftless, wan and dull production, desperate to say something new and original, but coming out as dislocated and incongruent.  The most annoying Cupid truly ruins the great aria of the Count and the whole opera \\"Vedr&ograve; mentr'io sospiro\\" by all the acrobatic routine of a pest. The music which is actually written in the style of opera seria, an expression of aristocratic grandeur and hauteur, inspired Beethoven for his \\"Waldstein\\" sonata, dedicated to the Count von Waldstein.  But here it did not play to what it amasses - the singer Bo Skovhus spent some of his energy on that petty fight with Cupid, so irrational in this particular place, too - as the idea of the aria is not really an amoroso one, but of a general power of a lord over his subjects.  This is probably the most glaring example of the bad, pretentious production, which was looking for clues in a wrong place - why did they not look in Mozart, da Ponte and Beaumarchais contemporaries???<br /><br />The whole atmosphere of sensual innuendo, the naughty amusement, the funny mischief of the ambiguous libertine spirit of the time when the work was created and performed - all that is absent, mortified by Freud and Strindberg macabre shadows, and even the color palette of this spectacle is grey and blue, fleshless and withered.  It is an extremely serious opera, with no trace of goofy buffa, and it lacks the luster of a heartfelt comedy.<br /><br />Countess Almaviva here is an unattractive woman, which also simplifies the idea of a licentious libertine who gets bored from a prey already captured, no matter how beautiful or ugly.  I think a beautiful Countess, like Kiri te Kanawa, would be much more convincing.  Besides, Dorothea Roschmann has too much vibrato, although overall her singing is very good.<br /><br />The production put action accents in wrong places, too - the annoying adult awkward Cupid is all over in scenes where maximum attention needs to be called by the music itself, to the unbreakable concentration on the arias; but in a place where an action and a show is invited,  it was stunningly static!  During the famous March in Act III Finale - \\"Ecco la marcia, andiamo\\", there was no movement at all on stage!  What a bizarre solution again!  Perhaps it was deliberately made to annoy the spectator, in the great neurotic tradition of Freud and Strindberg.<br /><br />Along the same lines of ugliness and modern preferences for the crude and repulsive, Marcellina and Bartolo made old wrecks, not funny at all, she sang very poorly, in a cracked voice, maybe on purpose but it was painful to listen; Bartolo is in the wheelchair, why?  His \\"La Vendetta\\" aria does not dance in such a mise-en-scene.<br /><br />Costumes are simply boring, showing lack of that creativity and imagination that permeates Mozart's music.<br /><br />Last October there was a similar \\"Le Nozze\\" in Teatro La Fenice in Venice, it went even further in the same direction as this one,  at times becoming truly outrageous, but it was much more entertaining, in a way.  Seeing my videos from it now, I think it was overall a more interesting interpretation than this one, lest for the extremism in some of its scenes, with spanking, hair pulling, whipping, raping and finally the Countess jumping out of the window, like Tosca.  At least it was less monotonous, and deserved a lot of hearty boo from the scandalized public, but it did generate some string reaction, while this production bores into the end.<br /><br />Overall, this performance is worthwhile, mostly musically; it does not bring out the whole visual potential of such a great laugh and witty entertainment as \\"Le Nozze\\";  there is a caf&eacute; \\"Figaro\\" in Mozarthaus in Vienna, but as this production, it does not do a true justice to the greatness of this opera.his production affected his performance in a negative way; in any case, I wish to see a swaggering, proud Count - then he is amusing in his miseries; but this image is just a hopeless loser, which is not too entertaining. B004LROUW2|4.0|Such a long read, so much detail on mundane everyday experiences, repetition of thoughts and scenes, jumps in reality that seem too readily accepted by the characters and an ending that leaves many of the plots arcs hanging, yeah that's the style of Haruki Murakami and it is a challenge to take on.  But if you will allow this book to settle into your reading it becomes an escape from those very things that make up our lives.  The things we daily lose significance of such as the pleasure of making a small meal for yourself with all the smells, tastes and touches of the process, the need to express how we feel to those who have made important impacts on our lives and the desire to stay grounded to what you believe to be right in the midst of a changing culture filled with difficulty, terror and incredibly hard choices of what is good and what is evil.  This is what a work of fiction does to a reader from the hand of a master of the genre and what it is meant to be at it's core.
